        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acsami.3c12815"><meta property="og:title" content="Disclosing the Interfacial Electrolyte Structure of Na-Insertion Electrode Materials: Origins of the Desolvation Phenomenon"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c12815/asset/images/acsami.3c12815.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="Among a variety of promising cathode materials for Na-ion batteries, polyanionic Na-insertion compounds are among the preferred choices due to known fast sodium transfer through the ion channels along their framework structures. The most interesting representatives are Na3V2(PO4)3 (NVP) and Na3V2(PO4)2F3 (NVPF), which display large Na+ diffusion coefficients (up to 10–9 m2 s–1 in NVP) and high voltage plateaux (up to 4.2 V for NVPF). While the diffusion in the solid material is well-known to be the rate-limiting step during charging, already being thoroughly discussed in the literature, interfacial transport of sodium ions from the liquid electrolyte toward the electrode was recently shown to be important due to complex ion desolvation effects at the surface. In order to fill the blanks in the description of the electrode/electrolyte interface in Na-ion batteries, we performed a molecular dynamics study of the local nanostructure of a series of carbonate-based sodium electrolytes at the NVP and the NVPF interfaces along with careful examination of the desolvation phenomenon. We show that the tightness of solvent packing at the electrode surface is a major factor determining the height of the free energy barrier associated with desolvation, which explains the differences between the NVP and the NVPF structures. To rationalize and emphasize the remarkable properties of this family of cathode materials, a complementary comparative analysis of the same electrolyte system at the carbon electrode interface was also performed."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c12815/asset/images/acsami.3c12815.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/1122814~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="931883a74ad354b8-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="931883a74ad354b8-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: 'e163767a-77a1-4577-8cbb-cb2d2f3adbb8'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="931883a74ad354b8-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'applied_materials_interfaces';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

        </div>
    

</div><div class="col-lg-9 col-md-8 col-sm-7 header_topbar_right">

class="article--latest"><div class="figure-viewer"><div class="figure-viewer_row"><div class="figure-viewer_col loop-trap"><div aria-live="polite" aria-atomic="true" class="figure-viewer_modal"><a <script nonce="931883a74ad354b8-SJC">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "aamick";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Disclosing the Interfacial Electrolyte Structure of Na-Insertion Electrode Materials: Origins of the Desolvation Phenomenon", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.3c12815", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Disclosing the Interfacial Electrolyte Structure of Na-Insertion Electrode Materials: Origins of the Desolvation Phenomenon", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.3c12815", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Disclosing the Interfacial Electrolyte Structure of Na-Insertion Electrode Materials: Origins of the Desolvation Phenomenon", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.3c12815", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Disclosing the Interfacial Electrolyte Structure of Na-Insertion Electrode Materials: Origins of the Desolvation Phenomenon", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.3c12815", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Disclosing the Interfacial Electrolyte Structure of Na-Insertion Electrode Materials: Origins of the Desolvation Phenomenon", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.3c12815", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Disclosing the Interfacial Electrolyte Structure of Na-Insertion Electrode Materials: Origins of the Desolvation Phenomenon", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.3c12815", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Disclosing the Interfacial Electrolyte Structure of Na-Insertion Electrode Materials: Origins of the Desolvation Phenomenon", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.3c12815", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Disclosing the Interfacial Electrolyte Structure of Na-Insertion Electrode Materials: Origins of the Desolvation Phenomenon", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.3c12815", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Disclosing the Interfacial Electrolyte Structure of Na-Insertion Electrode Materials: Origins of the Desolvation Phenomenon", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.3c12815", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

class="articleMetrics_count"><h2>Citations</h2><div class="articleMetrics-val"><a href="#citeThis" class="internalNav">1</a></div></div></div></div><div class="articleMetrics_about"><div class="base data-db-target-of="aboutArticleMetrics" id="about-article-metrics" class="dropBlock__holder"><button class="articleMetrics_close"> <i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-close_thin"></i><span class="sr-only">close</span></button><p>Article Views are the COUNTER-compliant sum of full text article downloads since November 2008 (both PDF and HTML) across all institutions and individuals. These metrics are regularly updated to reflect usage leading up to the last few days.</p><p>Citations are the number of other articles citing this article, calculated by Crossref and updated daily.<a href="https://www.crossref.org/services/cited-by/"> Find more information about Crossref citation counts.</a></p><p>The Altmetric Attention Score is a quantitative measure of the attention that a research article has received online. Clicking on the donut icon will load a page at altmetric.com with additional details about the score and the social media presence for the given article. Find more information on<a href="https://help.altmetric.com/support/solutions/articles/6000233311-how-is-the-altmetric-attention-score-calculated"> the Altmetric Attention Score and how the score is calculated.</a></p></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c12815/asset/images/medium/am3c12815_0008.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c12815/asset/images/large/am3c12815_0008.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.3c12815&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Among a variety of promising cathode materials for Na-ion batteries, polyanionic Na-insertion compounds are among the preferred choices due to known fast sodium transfer through the ion channels along their framework structures. The most interesting representatives are Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP) and Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub> (NVPF), which display large Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients (up to 10<sup>–9</sup> m<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup> in NVP) and high voltage plateaux (up to 4.2 V for NVPF). While the diffusion in the solid material is well-known to be the rate-limiting step during charging, already being thoroughly discussed in the literature, interfacial transport of sodium ions from the liquid electrolyte toward the electrode was recently shown to be important due to complex ion desolvation effects at the surface. In order to fill the blanks in the description of the electrode/electrolyte interface in Na-ion batteries, we performed a molecular dynamics study of the local nanostructure of a series of carbonate-based sodium electrolytes at the NVP and the NVPF interfaces along with careful examination of the desolvation phenomenon. We show that the tightness of solvent packing at the electrode surface is a major factor determining the height of the free energy barrier associated with desolvation, which explains the differences between the NVP and the NVPF structures. To rationalize and emphasize the remarkable properties of this family of cathode materials, a complementary comparative analysis of the same electrolyte system at the carbon electrode interface was also performed.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D59380%26pageCount%3D9%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DKateryna%2BGoloviznina%252C%2BEzzoubair%2BBendadesse%252C%2BOzlem%2BSel%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D15%26issueNum%3D51%26contentID%3Dacsami.3c12815%26title%3DDisclosing%2Bthe%2BInterfacial%2BElectrolyte%2BStructure%2Bof%2BNa-Insertion%2BElectrode%2BMaterials%253A%2BOrigins%2Bof%2Bthe%2BDesolvation%2BPhenomenon%26numPages%3D9%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D59388%26publicationDate%3DDecember%2B2023" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2023 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">

                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Desolvation&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_desolvation">desolvation</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Na-insertion+Electrode&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_Na-insertion%20electrode">Na-insertion electrode</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Na-ion+Battery&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_Na-ion%20battery">Na-ion battery</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Molecular+Dynamics&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_molecular%20dynamics">molecular dynamics</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=NASICON&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_NASICON">NASICON</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">1.  Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Being commercialized for about 25 years, Li-ion batteries (LIBs) have resulted in a revolution in energy-storage devices. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2 ref3" aria-label="reference 1 to 3">(1−3)</a> However, due to a constantly increasing demand in the number of devices and growth of the required performance, which is confronted by supply constraints, the development of alternatives emerges as a state-of-the-art direction of battery research. An appealing option is to use Na-ion batteries (SIBs) that possess similar characteristics as Li-based analogues, being notably cheaper thanks to the high natural abundance of sodium. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4 ref5 ref6 ref7" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref4 ref5 ref6 ref7" aria-label="reference 4 to 7">(4−7)</a> However, the transition from LIBs to SIBs brings new challenges in battery design requiring developments of the cathode and anode materials as well as adjustments in the electrolyte composition. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8 ref9" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref8 ref9" aria-label="reference 8 and 9">(8,9)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">NaSICON materials, which have a general formula of Na<sub><i>a</i></sub>M<sub><i>b</i></sub>(XO<sub>4</sub>)<sub><i>c</i></sub> (where M = Fe, V, Ti, Zr, etc., X = S, P, Si, etc.), <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10 ref11" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref10 ref11" aria-label="reference 10 and 11">(10,11)</a> being a subclass of polyanionic Na-insertion compounds, demonstrate fast Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion in “skeleton” structures <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref12" aria-label="reference 12">(12)</a> that make them powerful cathode materials for SIBs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref13" aria-label="reference 13">(13)</a> Their advantages include a high redox potential due to the unique inductive effect of the polyanionic groups, high thermal stability, and robustness of the structural framework during intercalation/deintercalation processes that guarantees long life cycling, while as a main drawback, their low electrical conductivity is considered. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4 ref5 ref14" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref4 ref5 ref14" aria-label="reference 4, 5 and 14">(4,5,14)</a> The most common representative is Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP), which has a theoretical capacity of 117 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> and a plateau at 3.4 V corresponding to the V<sup>3+</sup>/V<sup>4+</sup> redox reaction. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref15" aria-label="reference 15">(15)</a> Going beyond the NaSICON family, a further increase of the redox potential in the Na-insertion electrode compounds can be achieved by introducing electronegative anions, such as F<sup>–</sup>, OH<sup>–</sup>, etc., into the NVP, which perturb the covalent bond between the transition metal and oxygen atoms. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref14" aria-label="reference 14">(14)</a> Therefore, by replacing one phosphate group with fluorine anions, forming Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub> (NVPF), one can attain two high-voltage plateaus at 3.6 and 4.2 V. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref16" aria-label="reference 16">(16)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">The choice of the electrolyte remains crucial for the performance of SIBs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17 ref18" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref17 ref18" aria-label="reference 17 and 18">(17,18)</a> The most common way is to use a solution of sodium salt in an organic solvent, following the strategy of LIBs, though flammability and lack of safety stay an issue. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref19" aria-label="reference 19">(19)</a> A typical example is a NaPF<sub>6</sub>, NaClO<sub>4</sub>, or NaBF<sub>4</sub> salt dissolved in a carbonate solvent. Cyclic carbonates such as propylene carbonate (PC) or ethylene carbonate (EC) have a relatively high dielectric constant that favors the dissociation of a salt, while their linear analogues such as dimethyl carbonate (DMC) are often used as cosolvents to reduce the viscosity and ameliorate ionic conductivity of a system. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref20" aria-label="reference 20">(20)</a> The carbonates remain the first choice also because of the formation of a highly stable solid-electrolyte interphase (SEI) that protects the anode from a direct interaction with the solution. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7 ref21" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref7 ref21" aria-label="reference 7 and 21">(7,21)</a> Alternatively, ionic liquids <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref22" aria-label="reference 22">(22)</a> and aqueous <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref23" aria-label="reference 23">(23)</a> electrolytes can be used, but they do not lack disadvantages: the ILs usually possess high viscosities compared to conventional organic solvents that tend to increase even further upon the addition of a salt, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref24" aria-label="reference 24">(24)</a> while the aqueous electrolytes have a narrow voltage stability window. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref25" aria-label="reference 25">(25)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">While the majority of studies focus on the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion in the Na-insertion materials and the unit cell breathing upon intercalation, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26 ref27 ref28 ref29 ref30 ref31" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref26 ref27 ref28 ref29 ref30 ref31" aria-label="reference 26 to 31">(26−31)</a> the cathode–electrolyte interfacial structure remains underinvestigated. Nevertheless, the arrangement of the solvent at the electrode surface affects ion desolvation and its migration from the bulk toward the interface, known to be an important energy-consuming step. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32 ref33" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref32 ref33" aria-label="reference 32 and 33">(32,33)</a> For example, the energy barrier of Li<sup>+</sup> desolvation in an interphase-free LiPF<sub>6</sub>/EC-DMC/Li<sub>4</sub>Ti<sub>5</sub>O<sub>12</sub> measured by electrochemical impedance spectroscopy reaches about 12 kcal mol<sup>–1</sup>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a> Of course, extensive experimental studies are barely possible for scanning a vast range of electrolyte compositions combined with different electrode types, and theoretical methods such as classical molecular dynamics (MD) are indispensable for the rational design of electrode–electrolyte systems. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a></div><div class="NLM_p last">In a recent work, we combined electrochemical quartz crystal microbalance and operando infrared fiber evanescent wave spectroscopy with MD to study the operation of Na-insertion material-based SIBs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a> We showed that upon insertion, a depletion of desolvated Na<sup>+</sup> occurs at the interface, which can impact the rate capability of the batteries and may be generic to other types of solvent/ionic species. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a> Here, we complement this work by using extensive MD simulations. We shed light on the local structure of the 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> electrolyte in several organic carbonates [DMC, PC, and EC-DMC mixture (1:1 by molar fraction)] at the NVP and NVPF electrode surfaces, which will be compared to that at a graphite electrode. Note that although SIBs generally employ hard carbon as the anode, the local structure of graphite provides a good model for the interface with the electrolyte. We demonstrate that the packing of a solvent at an electrode surface due to solvent–electrode interactions appears as one of the determining factors defining the activation barrier of the desolvation process. In particular, the simulations allow us to determine the potential of mean force (PMF) experienced by the Na<sup>+</sup> ions to migrate from the liquid to the surface and to link it with the structural aspects, in particular, with the coordination number of solvent and anion species around them.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">2.  Methods</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Molecular dynamics simulations were performed using the LAMMPS program. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> Initial configurations and input files were generated using Packmol, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a> VESTA, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> and fftool <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref41" aria-label="reference 41">(41)</a> utilities. The simulation box consisted of two electrodes, separated by a 1 M solution of NaPF<sub>6</sub> in DMC, EC-DMC (1:1 molar fraction), or PC solvent. As electrode sets, graphite–graphite, NVP-Au, and NVPF-Au pairs were considered. In total, 9 different systems were modeled. An example of a simulation box is given in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c12815/asset/images/medium/am3c12815_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c12815/asset/images/large/am3c12815_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. Simulation snapshot of the 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>/DMC electrolyte in contact with the NVP (left) and gold (right) electrodes.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c12815/asset/images/large/am3c12815_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.3c12815&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The graphite electrode (GR) was represented by a hexagonal P6<sub>3</sub>/<i>mmc</i> space group <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a> and consisted of 3 layers of 34.08 × 36.89 Å size each. The NVP electrode was represented by a rhombohedral <i>R</i>3̅<i>c</i> space group, which is mostly always reported in the literature, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43 ref44 ref45" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref43 ref44 ref45" aria-label="reference 43 to 45">(43−45)</a> though Masquelier et al. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26 ref31" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref26 ref31" aria-label="reference 26 and 31">(26,31)</a> mention several times the existence of a monoclinic distortion at ambient temperature. The slab had a size of 34.92 × 30.24 × 21.65 Å. The [001] plane of NVP was exposed toward the solution, following the recommendations from the literature. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref26" aria-label="reference 26">(26)</a> The crystal structure of the NVP comprised two types of Na<sup>+</sup> ions, shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>a, the occupancy of which varied from one reference to another. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26 ref43 ref44 ref45" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref26 ref43 ref44 ref45" aria-label="reference 26 and 43 to 45">(26,43−45)</a> In order to match the experimental composition, we removed Na1 and Na2 ions only from the bulk of the electrode and not from the surface, which allowed us to avoid the intercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> ions from the solution to the solid. The NVPF electrode was represented by a tetragonal P4<sub>2</sub>/<i>mnm</i> space group, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref46" aria-label="reference 46">(46)</a> with the [001] plane exposed toward the solution, similarly to the NVP case. The slab had a size of 36.19 × 36.19 × 19.96 Å. This leads to 9.8 O and 4.9 F atoms per 1 nm<sup>2</sup> of the NVPF surface, which gives the O/F ratio of 2. The crystal structure contained two types of Na<sup>+</sup> ions, with occupancy of 1.0 for Na1 and 0.5 for Na2, respectively (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>b (left)). Since the distance of the closest Na2 sites is too short (1.865 Å), two neighboring Na2 sites cannot be simultaneously occupied due to the strong Coulomb repulsion between Na<sup>+</sup> ions. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref47" aria-label="reference 47">(47)</a> Therefore, we had to remove half of the Na2 atoms from the NVPF surface, and the resulting structure is given in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>b (right). In all cases, a gold counter electrode was used. It was represented by a cubic <i>Fm</i>3̅<i>m</i> space group <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref48" aria-label="reference 48">(48)</a> with the [100] plane exposed to the solvent. The slab had a thickness of 12.22 Å, while its <i>x</i> and <i>y</i> dimensions were adjusted to fit those of NVP or NVPF.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c12815/asset/images/medium/am3c12815_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c12815/asset/images/large/am3c12815_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. Visualization of the NVP (a) and NVPF (b) surfaces exposed to a solution. Gray and purple spheres represent the Na<sup>+</sup> ions with different occupations (denoted as Na1 and Na2). In the case of NVP, no Na1 and Na2 ions were removed from the surface. Instead, they were removed from the bulk electrode to match the corresponding occupation (not shown). In the case of NVPF, all Na1 ions were kept, and one of Na2 in each pair (b, left) was removed for the surface (b, right).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c12815/asset/images/large/am3c12815_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.3c12815&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The liquid phase contained 75 ion pairs of NaPF<sub>6</sub> salt and 845 molecules of DMC, 830 molecules of PC or 465 molecules of DMC plus 465 molecules of EC, matching the 1 M salt concentration. The box size in the <i>z</i> direction was adjusted based on the electrode type used, resulting in a 100–120 Å solution thickness. The total system size was approximately 15,000 atoms.</div><div class="NLM_p">The Na<sup>+</sup> cation was represented by the Aqvist force field, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref49" aria-label="reference 49">(49)</a> while the parameters of the PF<sub>6</sub><sup>–</sup> anion were taken from the CL&amp;P model. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref50" aria-label="reference 50">(50)</a> The net charge of the ions was kept an integer. The intermolecular interaction parameters of the electrode atoms, given in <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">Table 1</a>, were considered from several sources. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49 ref51 ref52 ref53 ref54 ref55" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref49 ref51 ref52 ref53 ref54 ref55" aria-label="reference 49 and 51 to 55">(49,51−55)</a> The bonded and nonbonded parameters of solvent atoms were taken from the OPLS-AA, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref56" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref56" aria-label="reference 56">(56)</a> except for the partial charges, which were computed using the CHelpG procedure <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref57" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref57" aria-label="reference 57">(57)</a> on electron densities obtained at the MP2/cc-pVTZ level of theory at previously optimized geometries with the Gaussian16 program. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref58" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref58" aria-label="reference 58">(58)</a> The solvent models were validated by computing the densities and viscosities of bulk 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> solutions in the DMC, EC-DMC, and PC systems, which were compared with the experimental values. The good reproduction of the viscosities shows that it is not mandatory to take into account explicit polarization effects, contrarily to the case of ionic liquids.</div><div class="NLM_table-wrap" id="tbl1"><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption"><div class="NLM_caption" id=""><div class="title2">Table 1. Non-Bonded Interaction Parameters of Electrodes</div></div></div><div class="scrollable-table-wrap" style="display: block;"><table class="table "><colgroup><col align="left"><col align="left"><col
