class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">V</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">+3</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">2.673</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">0.0602</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left"> <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref52" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref52" aria-label="reference 52">(52)</a></td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">&nbsp;</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">P</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">+5</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">3.740</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">0.1400</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left"> <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref53" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref53" aria-label="reference 53">(53)</a></td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">&nbsp;</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">O</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">–2</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">3.150</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">0.1700</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left"> <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref53" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref53" aria-label="reference 53">(53)</a></td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">&nbsp;</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">F</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">–1</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">3.440</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">0.4600</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left"> <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref54" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref54" aria-label="reference 54">(54)</a></td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">gold</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">Au</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">0</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">2.951</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">22.1334</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left"> <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref55" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref55" aria-label="reference 55">(55)</a></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div><div class="NLM_p">The simulation box was periodic in the <i>x</i> and <i>y</i> directions and a slab of 3 × <i>L</i><sub><i>z</i></sub> was considered along the <i>z</i> direction. A cutoff of 12 Å was applied for nonbonded interactions. The particle–particle particle–mesh method was used to evaluate electrostatic energies (accuracy of 10<sup>–5</sup>), with the slab correction taken into account. Both electrodes were considered immobile. Bonds terminating with hydrogen atoms were constrained using the SHAKE algorithm. The time step was set to 1 fs. The Nosé–Hoover thermostat was used to keep the temperature of the electrolyte solution at 298 K. Each system was equilibrated for 5 ns in the <i>NVT</i> ensemble and then a 50 ns production run was performed. The data analysis was performed using TRAVIS <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref59" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref59" aria-label="reference 59">(59)</a> and our self-written tools. The visualization of the simulation box was done in VMD. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref60" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref60" aria-label="reference 60">(60)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">The PMFs were calculated from separate runs, during which a single Na<sup>+</sup> ion was pulled from the solution toward the electrode surface. For this, a harmonic biasing potential varying between 25 and 150 kcal mol<sup>–1</sup> Å<sup>–2</sup> was applied to this Na<sup>+</sup> ion. The <i>z</i> distance between the ion and the top surface atoms (C for graphite and Na for NVP and NVPF) was considered as the biasing collective variable. It was sampled between 12 and −2 Å with a step of 0.25 Å with 100 ps equilibration and 400 ps acquisition at each separation. The PMFs were calculated using umbrella sampling and the weighed-histogram analysis method. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref61 ref62" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref61 ref62" aria-label="reference 61 and 62">(61,62)</a></div><div class="NLM_p last">From the PMF trajectories, the coordination numbers of Na<sup>+</sup> as a function of its distance from an electrode were calculated. The coordination number cutoff was set to 4.55 Å for P of PF<sub>6</sub><sup>–</sup> and 3.20 Å for O atoms of the carbonyl group of carbonate molecules, being defined as the position of the first minimum of the corresponding radial distribution functions of bulk systems. The coordination number curves were averaged over 3–6 replicas.</div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i5">3.  Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">We first analyze the structure adopted by the solvent molecules at the various interfaces. Density profiles (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>), which provide information about the probability of finding species as a function of their distance from an electrode, show that the local arrangement of a solvent at the graphite, NVP, and NVPF surfaces is considerably different. Being a nonpolar material with a flat homogeneous surface, graphite seems not to have strong interactions with any of the solvents. Oxygen atoms of both DMC and EC of the EC-DMC mixture can be found at about 3.2 and 6.6 Å from the graphite surface, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>a, which is consistent with neat DMC and PC (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>b). The behavior of a carbonate solvent at a given interface seems to be determined by its flexibility (linear DMC or cyclic EC and PC) and is almost not affected by the presence of a cosolvent. The orientation of the molecules with respect to the surface is analyzed by computing the angle α formed by the CO vector of a solvent and the normal vector to the electrode plane as a function of the distance of the α from the electrode surface. In the solvent mixture, DMC tends to expose its methyl groups to the graphite surface (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c12815/suppl_file/am3c12815_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1a</a>), so that the CO vector points away from the surface, marked by a greenish spot at about 4.8 Å O-surface separation (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a). This peak can be attributed to the solvent–electrolyte interactions as has been proposed by Borodin et al. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref63" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref63" aria-label="reference 63">(63)</a> in their study of LiPF<sub>6</sub>/EC-DMC/graphite by the surface-sensitive Ångstrom resolution X-ray reflectivity and molecular dynamics simulations. A lower probability but wide spot is observed at about 3.2 Å, which corresponds to the parallel orientation of the CO vector with respect to the surface plane. The ratio between these two peaks should depend on the electrolyte concentration in the solution. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref63" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref63" aria-label="reference 63">(63)</a> The second and further solvent layers are not clearly marked due to the conformational flexibility of DMC, which results in a variety of possible orientations. The behavior of the neat DMC solvent appears to be quite similar (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c12815/suppl_file/am3c12815_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2a</a>). On the contrary, the EC molecules of the mixture are mainly oriented parallel to the surface (corresponding to α = 90°, shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>b), at the O···surface separation of 3.2 Å. In addition to this, a low probably perpendicular orientation is observed at a 6.6 Å distance. In the case of neat PC (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c12815/suppl_file/am3c12815_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S2b</a>), this perpendicular orientation does not emerge, being replaced by two peaks at 6.5–7.5 Å with a tilted orientation of the CO vector. The second solvent layer of parallelly oriented carbonate molecules is well-defined for both EC in the mixture and the neat PC.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c12815/asset/images/medium/am3c12815_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c12815/asset/images/large/am3c12815_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Density profiles of O atoms (of the CO carbonyl group) of solvents in a 1 M solution of NaPF<sub>6</sub> in the EC-DMC mixture (a,c,e) and DMC, PC solvents (b,d,f) as a function of their distance from the graphite (a,b), NVP (c,d), and NVPF (e,f) electrodes. The top carbon layer of graphite was considered as <i>z</i> = 0 for (a,b), and the top sodium ions of the NVP and of the NVPF for (c,d) and (e,f), respectively. The insets of (c,e) illustrate the definition of <i>z</i> = 0 for the NVP and NVPF electrodes. The solvent structures given in the insets of (a,b) highlight the atoms for which the density profiles were computed. Note that they do not represent any particular configuration of a solvent at a surface and are used only for illustrative purposes.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c12815/asset/images/large/am3c12815_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.3c12815&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c12815/asset/images/medium/am3c12815_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c12815/asset/images/large/am3c12815_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Probability contours revealing the orientation of the DMC (a,c,e) and EC (b,d,f) components in the 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>/EC-DMC solution with respect to the electrode surface. The <i>x</i>-axes represent the distances between the electrode (the top C for the graphite and the top Na for the NVP and NVPF) and the O atoms of the carbonyl group of a solvent. The <i>y</i>-axis represents the angles formed by the normal vector to an electrode plane and the CO vector of a solvent, as illustrated in the inset.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c12815/asset/images/large/am3c12815_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.3c12815&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The arrangement of solvent molecules at the NVP surface is strikingly different from that at graphite. The NVP slab, used for simulations, was cut in a way so that the PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3–</sup> and the Na<sup>+</sup> ions are exposed to a solution. We considered it the most likely case because the direction of the highest Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion in the solid <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref26" aria-label="reference 26">(26)</a> is now aligned with the <i>z</i> axis of our simulation box. The top sodium ions of the NVP are then considered as a reference (<i>z</i> = 0) for the corresponding density profiles. A prominent peak between the top Na<sup>+</sup> ions of the surface and the top O atoms of the carbonyl groups is observed at about 2.15 Å in all solvents (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>c–d). The high intensity of this peak points to a tight packing of the solvent molecules near the electrode. The orientation of the CO vector with respect to the surface plane is strictly perpendicular (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 4</a>c,d and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c12815/suppl_file/am3c12815_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S3c,d</a>). For DMC in the EC-DMC mixture, several low-intense spots occur at 4.82, 6.2, and 7.15 Å O···surface separations with different α values. On the contrary, in neat DMC, among these low-intensity contributions, only that at 7.15 Å with CO vector pointing away from the NVP plane is present. The probability of this peak increases when a cyclic carbonate is considered instead of a linear one: the intensity ratio between the peaks at 2.15 and 7.15 Å decreases from 17 for DMC to 2 for EC in the mixture. A similar effect was observed by Smith et al. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref64" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref64" aria-label="reference 64">(64)</a> when modeling the LiPF<sub>6</sub>/EC-DMC/LiFePO<sub>4</sub> system: DMC molecules showed a slightly higher probability of interacting with alkali metal cations of the electrode, while EC molecules directed some of their hydrogen atoms toward the O atoms at the surface.</div><div class="NLM_p">Due to the presence of F atoms at the interface, the NVPF electrode appears to be more solvophobic than the NVP. The solvent molecules still interact with the top Na<sup>+</sup> ions, but the intensities of corresponding peaks are lower (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>e,f). The DMC molecules are preferably oriented perpendicularly to the surface (α is close to 180°, <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>e): however, in the case of neat liquid (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c12815/suppl_file/am3c12815_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3e</a>), the CO is tilted from being completely perpendicular by about 15°. The latter allows the methyl groups of the solvent to undergo weak dispersion interactions with the F atoms at the surface. The cyclic carbonates exhibit three possible orientations (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 4</a>f and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c12815/suppl_file/am3c12815_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S3f</a>): the first one is perpendicular to the electrode, located at a characteristic distance of 2.1 Å, while the second one is further away from the electrode at an average distance of 6.1 Å. The third orientation is tilted, with α = 45° at 4.2 Å. The ratio between these orientations depends on the presence of a cosolvent. In the EC-DMC mixture, we observe a clear role splitting: while the linear carbonate interacts with top Na<sup>+</sup> ions of the surface, the CO bond of the cyclic one is orientated toward the bulk solution. In the neat PC (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c12815/suppl_file/am3c12815_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3f</a>), both roles are played by PC molecules in equal proportions [evaluated by integrating the corresponding <i>p</i>(<i>z</i>) peaks].</div><div class="NLM_p">As was mentioned before, this notable fraction of solvent molecules oriented toward the bulk solution can be explained by the interactions with the NaPF<sub>6</sub> salt. In the case of graphite (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>a), Na<sup>+</sup> ions are mainly found at about 7.1 Å from the electrode in the DMC and EC-DMC solutions and at 8.6 Å in PC, being followed by several low-intensity peaks at a larger separation. Since no tight solvent layer is present near the electrode, the Na<sup>+</sup> ions can approach the graphite even closer, being noticed at 4.4–4.8 Å separations. At the NVP electrode (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>b), which exhibits strong solvent adsorption, an intense Na<sup>+</sup> peak is observed just after the first solvent layer, at about 9.2–9.4 Å. In the case of neat DMC, it is split into three smaller peaks (at 8.3, 9.4, and 11.4 Å) due to multiple configurations of the flexible DMC molecule (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c12815/suppl_file/am3c12815_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3c</a>), but the integral under them match that in EC/DMC and PC. At the NVPF surface (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>c), the Na ions are distributed in the range of 6.8–13.6 Å separations, with a principal peak at about 8.2–8.6 Å due to several dominant orientations of solvent molecules in the first layer(<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 4</a>e–f and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c12815/suppl_file/am3c12815_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S3e,f</a>).</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c12815/asset/images/medium/am3c12815_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c12815/asset/images/large/am3c12815_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. Density profiles of Na<sup>+</sup> ions (a–c) and P atoms (d–f) of the 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> salt in DMC, PC solvents, and the EC-DMC mixture as a function of their distance from the graphite (a,d), NVP (b,e), and NVPF (c,f) electrodes. The top carbon layer of graphite and the top sodium ions of the NVP and NVPF are considered <i>z</i> = 0, similarly to <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c12815/asset/images/large/am3c12815_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.3c12815&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">While the sodium cation is small and densely charged, the PF<sub>6</sub><sup>–</sup> anion is remarkably larger; it thus displays a lower charge density. Therefore, it is mainly located just after the first solvent layer at about 7.7, 9.0, and 8.8 Å from the graphite, NVP, and NVPF surfaces, respectively (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>d–f). In all the solvents, the peak is high and well-defined due to the large anion size, which also allows us to clearly identify the second and sometimes third anionic layers, especially when neat rigid PC is used as a solvent. Similarly to the Na<sup>+</sup> cations, the PF<sub>6</sub><sup>–</sup> anions can also approach the graphite surface closer, down to distances of about 4.2 Å, while no similar effects are observed in the NVP and NVPF cases.</div><div class="NLM_p">The PMF, the free energy profile as a function of the distance of a Na<sup>+</sup> ion relative to the electrode surface, can provide complementary information to the solvent arrangement. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref65 ref66" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref65 ref66" aria-label="reference 65 and 66">(65,66)</a> Here, as a reference (<i>z</i> = 0), the distance corresponding to the minimum of each PMF is taken, being different from the density profile plots discussed earlier. Since there is no free energy barrier in approaching a Na<sup>+</sup> ion to the graphite surface in DMC or EC-DMC (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>a), it can easily penetrate the closest solvent layer to the surface. This observation is consistent with the works of Hwang et al. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref67" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref67" aria-label="reference 67">(67)</a> and Vatamanu et al. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref68" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref68" aria-label="reference 68">(68)</a> on the LiPF<sub>6</sub>/EC-DMC/graphite system. On the contrary, a barrier of about 4.5 kcal mol<sup>–1</sup> is observed in PC, probably related to a rigid geometry of the solvent molecules. This is reflected in the small prepeaks in density profiles, shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>a, when the probability of observing a Na<sup>+</sup> ion near the surface is lower in neat PC than in other solvents. Alternatively, the free energy barrier can be estimated from the density profiles as −<i>RT</i> log(<i>p</i>(<i>z</i>)). The obtained value of 6.5 kcal mol<sup>–1</sup> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c12815/suppl_file/am3c12815_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>) for the PC solution is in agreement with that computed earlier (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>a). Due to the absence of specific sites at the graphite surface, capable of attracting the cation, it can freely move along and across the surface and move back to the bulk solution. Only in the case of PC, a barrier of about 3 kcal mol<sup>–1</sup> should be overcome. In contrast to graphite, the NVP surface (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>b) tends to form a firm solvent layer. In all of the solvents, the free energy barrier attains about 20–25 kcal mol<sup>–1</sup>. Once a cation reaches the NVP surface, it stays trapped there, being coordinated by three oxygen atoms of neighboring PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3–</sup> groups, so that it is placed on the top of V atoms. The free energy of diffusing back to the solution appears to be too high (90–120 kcal mol<sup>–1</sup>) to conquer. However, we note that such an important barrier may result from the use of unscaled charge for the oxygen atoms at the surface in the force field, so that it should be taken as an upper bound value.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c12815/asset/images/medium/am3c12815_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c12815/asset/images/large/am3c12815_0006.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. PMF of approaching a single Na<sup>+</sup> ion to the graphite (a), NVP (b), and NVPF (c) electrodes in different solvents. The simulation snapshots (d–f) represent top and side views of the most energetically favorable position of the Na<sup>+</sup> ion at the graphite (d), NVP (e), and NVPF (f) surfaces, considered as <i>z</i> = 0 for (a–c), respectively.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c12815/asset/images/large/am3c12815_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.3c12815&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The solvent layer at the NVPF surface is less tight, and the energy barrier achieves only 7–10 kcal mol<sup>–1</sup>. The most probable adsorption site is in the hollow at the surface, being bound by two oxygen atoms of PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3–</sup> groups. In such a case, the cation starts to penetrate the electrode, and further investigation of this process becomes out of the scope of classical MD. Alternatively, Na<sup>+</sup> ions can be absorbed on the top of P atoms, stabilized by interaction with two oxygen atoms (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c12815/suppl_file/am3c12815_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>), when no hollow site is available nearby.</div><div class="NLM_p">The origin of the free energy barrier rises from the partial desolvation of a Na<sup>+</sup> ion when it approaches the electrode, penetrating through a tight solvent layer. This can be confirmed by tracking the coordination number of a single Na<sup>+</sup> ion moving toward the surface. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a> (as well as <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c12815/suppl_file/am3c12815_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>) shows that, indeed, no desolvation is observed for the graphite, while the Na<sup>+</sup> cation loses its solvation shell when approaching NVP and NVPF. When comparing different materials, the firmness of solvent packing at a carbonate-based solvent at the electrode surface is greater at the NVP than at the NVPF, which actually explains the difference in the energy barriers: highly oriented solvent molecules at the interface require a higher energy contribution to change their configuration to solvate the diffusing Na<sup>+</sup> ion. In our recent EQCM study of the same systems under electrochemical cycling, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a> we observed a detectable mass loss right before the insertion of Na<sup>+</sup> ions related to the desolvation only at the NVP, while in the case of NVPF, due to lose packing, the desolvation process remains confined within the penetration depth of the resonator’s acoustic wave and thus cannot be detected as a mass loss. When the carbonate series is compared, the difference in PMFs is not pronounced, but salt aggregation varies as a function of a solvent. Both in the DMC and EC-DMC mixture, the Na<sup>+</sup> ion moves as a contact ion pair or a solvent-separated ion pair, which can be seen from the cation–anion coordination numbers that are smaller than 1 (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c12815/suppl_file/am3c12815_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a>). In PC, in addition to contact ion pairs, some aggregates containing 2–3 pairs of ions are observed, since the cation–anion coordination number becomes 1.25.</div><figure id="fig7" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 7</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c12815/asset/images/medium/am3c12815_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c12815/asset/images/large/am3c12815_0007.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 7. Total coordination number of solvent and anion species around a Na<sup>+</sup> cation moving toward the electrode surface in the NaPF<sub>6</sub>/EC-DMC solution. As <i>z</i> = 0, the minimum of each PMF given in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a> is considered. The coordination number values are averaged over several replicas.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c12815/asset/images/large/am3c12815_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.3c12815&amp;id=fig7"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">Quantitative and even qualitative comparison with literature data remains problematic since the value of the activation barrier depends on a diversity of factors. In addition, no data for Na<sup>+</sup>-based systems have been reported to this point. Abe et al. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32 ref69" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref32 ref69" aria-label="reference 32 and 69">(32,69)</a> measured the activation barriers of LiClO<sub>4</sub> salt in DMC and EC-DMC (1:1 by vol.) at graphite by electrochemical impedance spectroscopy, and the variation between solvents (9.6 and 13.8 kcal mol<sup>–1</sup> for DMC and EC-DMC, respectively) was explained by the lower solvation ability of DMC than that of EC. However, changing the nature of the SEI almost vanished the difference between the two solvents. In our theoretical model, no SEI is present, and no barrier is observed for NaPF<sub>6</sub> in these two solvents near graphite. Nevertheless, the height of the free energy barrier (when present) should also depend on the nucleophilic properties of a solvent. In their experimental study of a SEI-free LiPF<sub>6</sub>/Li<sub>4</sub>Ti<sub>5</sub>O<sub>12</sub> system, Xu et al. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a> showed that an ether-based electrolyte had lower desolvation energy (by about 3 kcal mol<sup>–1</sup>) than carbonates. By modeling the diffusion of a Li<sup>+</sup> ion toward LiCoO<sub>2</sub> in PC and water, Nikitina et al. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref70" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref70" aria-label="reference 70">(70)</a> also obtained rather different values: 10.4 and 5.8 kcal mol<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. For our DMC/EC-DMC/PC carbonate series, the difference between solvents’ donor ability is too delicate to be captured by simulations: partial changes of O solvent atoms are quite close, and no explicit lone pairs are present. Therefore, the solvent packing at the interface appears to play a principal role in defining the height of the activation barrier.</div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i6">4.  Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">To sum up, the migration of a Na<sup>+</sup> ion through the electrolyte solution toward the cathode is strongly dependent on the surface nature. The nanostructure of the closest solvent layer at the interface defines the height of the activation barrier, commonly associated with the desolvation process. The desolvation does not seem to be observed at the SEI-free graphite electrode due to the absence of specific directional interaction between the solvent and the carbon sheets. On the contrary, because of the surface inhomogeneity and the presence of donor and acceptor groups, the polyanionic Na-insertion compounds exhibit a notable arrangement of solvent molecules in the closest layer, penetrating through which a Na<sup>+</sup> ion partially loses its solvent shell. The effect is the most pronounced at the NVP, which demonstrates the greatest energy barrier among the studied systems as a result of the highly ordered and well-oriented first solvent layer. The solvent arrangement at the NVPF surface is slightly less marked due to the presence of nonpolar fluorine atoms, which partially disturb the solvent–electrode electrostatic attraction.</div><div class="NLM_p last">The nature of the solvent also plays an important role but only when different families of solvents are compared. In our carbonate series, the variation between solvents (namely, due to the partial charge distribution) is quite subtle, being a less significant factor than the electrode material. Even so, the lack of flexibility of the solvent impacts the tightness of its packing at the surface, which can lead to an increase in the activation energy barrier and affects the salt aggregation in the solution.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i7">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsami.3c12815?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsami.3c12815</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Density profiles, probability contours, simulation snapshots, and coordination number plots (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c12815/suppl_file/am3c12815_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c12815/suppl_file/am3c12815_si_001.pdf">am3c12815_si_001.pdf (2.31 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.3c12815" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

A</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2019</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>7</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">2942</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">2964</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Lithium-ion batteries: outlook on present, future, and hybridized technologies</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kim, Taehoon; Song, Wentao; Son, Dae-Yong; Ono, Luis K.; Qi, Yabing</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">7</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">7</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2942-2964</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JMCAET</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2050-7496</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) continue to draw vast attention as a promising energy storage technol. due to their high energy d., low self-discharge property, nearly zero-memory effect, high open circuit voltage, and long lifespan.  In particular, high-energy d. lithium-ion batteries are considered as the ideal power source for elec. vehicles (EVs) and hybrid elec. vehicles (HEVs) in the automotive industry, in recent years.  This review discusses key aspects of the present and the future battery technologies on the basis of the working electrode.  We then discuss how lithium-ion batteries evolve to meet the growing demand on high charge capacity and electrode stability.  An account of a stand-alone energy device (off-grid system) that combines an energy harvesting technol. with a lithium-ion battery is also provided.  The main discussion is categorized into three perspectives such as the evolution from the conventional to the advanced LIBs (e.g., Li-rich transition metal oxide and Ni-rich transition metal oxide batteries), to the state-of-the-art LIBs (e.g., Li-air, Li-sulfur batteries, org. electrode batteries, solid-state batteries, and Li-CO2 batteries), and to the hybridized LIBs (e.g., metal halide perovskite batteries).</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref4" class="refNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit4" data-doi="10.1021/cr500192f"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yabuuchi, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kubota, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dahbi, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Komaba, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Research Development on Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Chem. Rev.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2014</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>114</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">11636</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">11682</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1021/cr500192f</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D114%26publication_year%3D2014%26pages%3D11636-11682%26journal%3DChem.%2BRev.%26author%3DN.%2BYabuuchi%26author%3DK.%2BKubota%26author%3DM.%2BDahbi%26author%3DS.%2BKomaba%26title%3DResearch%2BDevelopment%2Bon%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26doi%3D10.1021%252Fcr500192f&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.3c12815&amp;doiOfLink=10.1021%2Fcr500192f&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Research Development on Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yabuuchi, Naoaki; Kubota, Kei; Dahbi, Mouad; Komaba, Shinichi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Reviews (Washington, DC, United States)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2014</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">114</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">23</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">11636-11682</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CHREAY</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0009-2665</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review of sodium-ion battery technol.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The last 10 years established the beginning of a post-lithium era in the field of energy storage, with the renaissance of Na-ion batteries (NIBs) as alternative for Li-based systems.  The development of this technol. has required intense work in materials research in order to produce and optimize anodes, cathodes, and electrolytes for NIBs.  The strong and weak points of the main families of compds. for each battery component are analyzed in this progress report.  Taking into account the achievements made in materials for NIBs, the industrial scene is analyzed through the existing prototypes and com. cells and also from an economical viewpoint.  In this scenario, where Na-ion technol. seems to be ready for a coming second generation, the use of Na can be extended to almost the whole spectrum of electrochem. energy storage systems: the new room temp. Na-S systems, high-energy Na-air technol., or high-power Na-based hybrid supercapacitors.                         cas_cit7R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">7</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium-ion batteries: present and future</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hwang, Jang-Yeon; Myung, Seung-Taek; Sun, Yang-Kook</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Society Reviews</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">46</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">12</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3529-3614</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Energy prodn. and storage technologies have attracted a great deal of attention for day-to-day applications.  In recent decades, advances in lithium-ion battery (LIB) technol. have improved living conditions around the globe.  LIBs are used in most mobile electronic devices as well as in zero-emission electronic vehicles.  However, there are increasing concerns regarding load leveling of renewable energy sources and the smart grid as well as the sustainability of lithium sources due to their limited availability and consequent expected price increase.  Therefore, whether LIBs alone can satisfy the rising demand for small- and/or mid-to-large-format energy storage applications remains unclear.  To mitigate these issues, recent research has focused on alternative energy storage systems.  Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered as the best candidate power sources because sodium is widely available and exhibits similar chem. to that of LIBs; therefore, SIBs are promising next-generation alternatives.  Recently, sodiated layer transition metal oxides, phosphates and org. compds. have been introduced as cathode materials for SIBs.  Simultaneously, recent developments have been facilitated by the use of select carbonaceous materials, transition metal oxides (or sulfides), and intermetallic and org. compds. as anodes for SIBs.  Apart from electrode materials, suitable electrolytes, additives, and binders are equally important for the development of practical SIBs.  Despite developments in electrode materials and other components, there remain several challenges, including cell design and electrode balancing, in the application of sodium ion cells.  In this article, we summarize and discuss current research on materials and propose future directions for SIBs.  This will provide important insights into scientific and practical issues in the development of SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref8"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref8" class="refNumLink">8</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit8" data-doi="10.1002/anie.201703772"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nayak, P.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Mobile and stationary energy storage by rechargeable batteries is a topic of broad societal and economical relevance.  Lithium-ion battery (LIB) technol. is at the forefront of the development, but a massively growing market will likely put severe pressure on resources and supply chains.  Recently, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have been reconsidered with the aim of providing a lower-cost alternative that is less susceptible to resource and supply risks.  On paper, the replacement of lithium by sodium in a battery seems straightforward at first, but unpredictable surprises are often found in practice.  What happens when replacing lithium by sodium in electrode reactions.  This review provides a state-of-the art overview on the redox behavior of materials when used as electrodes in lithium-ion and sodium-ion batteries, resp.  Advantages and challenges related to the use of sodium instead of lithium are discussed.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref9"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref9" class="refNumLink">9</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit9" data-doi="10.1039/c2ee02781j"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Palomares, V.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Serras, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Villaluenga, I.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hueso, K.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Energy prodn. and storage have become key issues concerning our welfare in daily life.  Present challenges for batteries are twofold.  In the first place, the increasing demand for powering systems of portable electronic devices and zero-emission vehicles stimulates research towards high energy and high voltage systems.  In the second place, low cost batteries are required in order to advance towards smart elec. grids that integrate discontinuous energy flow from renewable sources, optimizing the performance of clean energy sources.  Na-ion batteries can be the key for the second point, because of the huge availability of sodium, its low price and the similarity of both Li and Na insertion chemistries.  In spite of the lower energy d. and voltage of Na-ion based technologies, they can be focused on applications where the wt. and footprint requirement is less drastic, such as elec. grid storage.  Much work has to be done in the field of Na-ion in order to catch up with Li-ion technol.  Cathodic and anodic materials must be optimized, and new electrolytes will be the key point for Na-ion success. NaSICON-based electrode materials are known for their wide range of electrochem. potentials, high ionic cond., and most importantly their structural and thermal stabilities.  Using first-principles calcns., we chart the chem. space of 3d transition metal-based NaSICON phosphates with the formula NaxMM'(PO4)3 (with M and M' = Ti, V, Cr, Mn, Fe, Co and Ni) to analyze their thermodn. stabilities and the intercalation voltages for Na+ ions.                         cas_cit13R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">13</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Na+-Activation Engineering in the Na3V2(PO4)3 Cathode with Boosting Kinetics for Fast-Charging Na-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Shen, Xing; Su, Yuefeng; Yang, Ni; Jiang, Xiaoping; Liu, Xingxing; Mo, Junlin; Ran, Yan; Wu, Feng</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na superionic conductor-structured phosphates have attracted wide interest due to their high working voltage and fast Na+ migration facilitated by the robust 3-dimensional open framework.  However, they usually suffer from low-rate capability and inferior cycling stability due to the low intrinsic electronic cond. and limited activated Na+ ions.  Herein, a doping protocol with Na+ in the V3+ site is developed to activate extra electrochem. Na+ ions and expand the migration path of Na+, leading to the improvement of the electronic cond. and diffusion kinetics.  It is also disclosed that the generated stronger Na-O bonds with high ionicity significantly conduce to the enhanced structural stability in the Na+-substituted Na3.05V1.975Na0.025(PO4)3/C cathode.  The obtained composite can deliver an excellent rate capacity of 83.8 mA h g-1 at 20 C and a moderate cycling persistence of 91.3% over 1500 cycles at 10 C with great fast-charging properties.  The reversible structure evolution is confirmed by the ex situ XRD, XPS, and ICP characterization.  This work sheds light on awakening electroactive Na+ ions and designing phosphates with superior electrochem. stability for practical Na-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref14"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref14" class="refNumLink">14</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit14" data-doi="10.1039/C9CS00846B"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jin, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, P.-F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jiao, L.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Polyanion-Type Cathode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Chem.                         cas_cit14R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">14</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Polyanion-type cathode materials for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Jin Ting; Li Huangxu; Zhu Kunjie; Wang Peng-Fei; Liu Pei; Jiao Lifang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Society reviews</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">49</span>

    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2342-2377</span>
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn"></span>.
    </div><div class="casAbstract">Room-temperature sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are regarded as promising candidates for smart grids and large-scale energy storage systems (EESs) due to their significant benefits of abundant and low-cost sodium resource.  Among the previously reported cathode materials for SIBs, layered transition-metal oxides and polyanion-type materials are considered to be the most attractive options.  Although many layered transition-metal oxides can provide high capacity due to their small molecular weight, their further application is hindered by low output voltage (mostly lower than 3.5 V), irreversible phase transition as well as storage instability.  Comparatively, polyanion-type materials exhibit higher operating potentials due to the inductive effect of polyanion groups.  Their robust 3D framework significantly decreases the structural variations during sodium ion de/intercalation.  Moreover, the effect of strong X-O (X = S, P, Si, etc.) covalent bonds can effectively inhibit oxygen evolution.  These advantages contribute to the superior cycle stability and high safety of polyanion-type materials.  However, low electronic conductivity and limited capacity still restrict their further application.  This review summarizes the recent progress of polyanion-type materials for SIBs, which include phosphates, fluorophosphates, pyrophosphates, mixed phosphates, sulfates, and silicates.  We also discuss the remaining challenges and corresponding strategies for polyanion-type materials.  We hope this review can provide some insights into the development of polyanionic materials.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref15"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref15" class="refNumLink">15</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit15" data-doi="10.1016/j.ceramint.2021.03.122"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cheng, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tian, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Linghu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tian, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">He, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, L.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Na3V2(PO4)3/C·Na3V2(PO4)2F3/C@rGO blended cathode material with elevated energy density for sodium ion batteries</span>. <i>Ceram.                         cas_cit15R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">15</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Na3V2(PO4)3/C.Na3V2(PO4)2F3/C@rGO blended cathode material with elevated energy density for sodium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Cheng, Jun; Chen, Yanjun; Sun, Shiqi; Tian, Zeyi; Linghu, Yaoyao; Tian, Zhen; Wang, Chao; He, Zhenfeng; Guo, Li</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Ceramics International</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2021</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">47</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">13</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">18065-18074</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CINNDH</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0272-8842</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na3V2(PO4)3 (NVP), a promising cathode material of sodium ion batteries, has gained much attention due to its competitive capability of sodium storage.  However, the relatively low discharge potential of ∼3.4 V (vs. Na+/Na) needs to be improved and the current energy d. cannot satisfy the practical applications.  Herein, the introduction of Na3V2(PO4)2F3 with high voltage plateaus is adopted to elevate the energy d.  Therefore, the mixed composite seems to be more attractive because of the prospective high energy character.  Nevertheless, this mixt. still suffers from the intrinsic poor electronic cond.  Accordingly, a novel Na3V2(PO4)3/C·Na3V2(PO4)2F3/C @reduced graphene oxide (rGO) (denoted as B@rGO) blended cathode material is successfully synthesized through a facile sol-gel method.  The coated carbon layers and rGO are employed to construct a conductive network for promoting the ionic and electronic diffusion efficiently.  Detailed kinetic analyses demonstrate that the NVP phase dominates the kinetic reaction in the blended composite.  With respect to the electrochem. properties, this modified B@rGO composite can deliver a discharge capacity of 115.1 mAh g-1 at 0.1 C, corresponding to a high energy d. of 418.3 Wh kg-1, which is far more than the theor. one of pure NVP (399.8 Wh kg-1).  Moreover, the cyclic characteristics are significantly improved, resulting from the boosted structural stability.  Even at a high rate of 30 C, it can release a reversible capacity of 85.9 mAh g-1, and a high capacity retention of 81.6% is obtained after 200 cycles.  Furthermore, the superior electrochem. properties are detd. and demonstrated by the improved kinetics, including the large capacitive contribution (79.6% at 0.5 mV s-1) and rapid ionic migration.  Therefore, this novel co-modification strategy with high-voltage material and conductive rGO can be an efficient way for Na3V2(PO4)3 to optimize the electrochem. performance and push its application in sodium ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref16"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref16" class="refNumLink">16</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit16" data-doi="10.1016/j.ensm.2019.09.014"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xie, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jiang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">High Performance Cathode Material Based on Na3V2(PO4)2F3 and Na3V2(PO4)3 for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Energy Storage Mater.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2020</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>25</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">724</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">730</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A comprehensive study is reported entailing optimization of sodium ion electrolyte formulation and compatibility studies with pos. and neg. electrode materials.  EC:PC:DMC and EC:PC:DME were found to exhibit optimum ionic conductivities and lower viscosities.  Yet, hard carbon neg. electrode materials tested in such electrolytes exhibit significant differences in performance, rooted in the different resistivity of the SEI, which results in too large polarization and concomitant loss of capacity at low potentials when DME is used as a co-solvent.                         cas_cit23R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">23</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Water-Locked Eutectic Electrolyte Enables Long-Cycling Aqueous Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Liu, Tingting; Wu, Han; Du, Xiaofan; Wang, Jinzhi; Chen, Zheng; Wang, Hao; Sun, Jinran; Zhang, Jianjun; Niu, Jiaping; Yao, Lishan; Zhao, Jingwen; Cui, Guanglei</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Aq. sodium batteries are one of the awaited technologies for large-scale energy storage, but remain poorly rechargeable because of the reactivity issues of water.  Here, we present a hydrated eutectic electrolyte featuring a water-locked effect, which is exceptional in that the O-H bond of water is essentially strengthened via weak hydrogen bonding (relative to the original H2O-H2O hydrogen bonds) to low-donor-no. anions and ligands.  Even without interphase protection, both the anodic and cathodic water electrodecompn. reactions are delayed, extending the aq. potential window to 3.4 V.  Combined with the alleviated electrode dissoln., Na2MnFe(CN)6||NaTi2(PO4)3 batteries deliver a high energy d. of ~ 80 W h kg-1 at 0.5 C and undergo over 1000 cycles with a 74.5% capacity retention and a 99.4% Coulombic efficiency at 4.2 C.  This work may offer a general guide to ultimately exploit the water's innate stability for realizing the promise of aq. battery technologies.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref24"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref24" class="refNumLink">24</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit24" data-doi="10.1039/C7CS00464H"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, X.-G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, Y.-S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xing, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dai, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Ionic Liquids and Derived Materials for Lithium And Sodium Batteries</span>. <i>Chem.                         cas_cit24R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">24</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Ionic liquids and derived materials for lithium and sodium batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yang, Qiwei; Zhang, Zhaoqiang; Sun, Xiao-Guang; Hu, Yong-Sheng; Xing, Huabin; Dai, Sheng</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Society Reviews</span>

        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">6</span>),

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The ever-growing demand for advanced energy storage devices in portable electronics, elec. vehicles and large scale power grids has triggered intensive research efforts over the past decade on lithium and sodium batteries.  The key to improve their electrochem. performance and enhance the service safety lies in the development of advanced electrode, electrolyte, and auxiliary materials.  Ionic liqs. (ILs) are liqs. consisting entirely of ions near room temp., and are characterized by many unique properties such as ultralow volatility, high ionic cond., good thermal stability, low flammability, a wide electrochem. window, and tunable polarity and basicity/acidity.  These properties create the possibilities of designing batteries with excellent safety, high energy/power d. and long-term stability, and also provide better ways to synthesize known materials.  IL-derived materials, such as poly(ionic liqs.), ionogels and IL-tethered nanoparticles, retain most of the characteristics of ILs while being endowed with other favorable features, and thus they have received a great deal of attention as well.  This review provides a comprehensive review of the various applications of ILs and derived materials in lithium and sodium batteries including Li/Na-ion, dual-ion, Li/Na-S and Li/Na-air (O2) batteries, with a particular emphasis on recent advances in the literature.  Their unique characteristics enable them to serve as advanced resources, medium, or ingredient for almost all the components of batteries, including electrodes, liq. electrolytes, solid electrolytes, artificial solid-electrolyte interphases, and current collectors.  Some thoughts on the emerging challenges and opportunities are also presented in this review for further development.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref25"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref25" class="refNumLink">25</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit25" data-doi="10.1021/cr500232y"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kim, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hong, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Park, K.-Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kim, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kim, S.-W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kang, K.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Aqueous Rechargeable Li and Na Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Chem. Its crystal structure was detd. from single-crystal X-ray diffraction in 1999 by Le Meins et al. in the tetragonal space group P42/mnm at 298 K.  In this work, we show how the use of very high angular resoln. synchrotron radiation diffraction reveals a subtle orthorhombic distortion with unit-cell parameters of a = 9.02847(3) Å, b = 9.04444(3) Å, c = 10.74666(6) Å in the Amam space group. Furthermore, the crystal structure of the high-temp. form of Na3V2(PO4)2F3 (at 400 K) was detd. for the first time.  This allowed comparing the totally disordered distribution of Na ions in this case with the partially ordered one of the room-temp. phase.  We report here on an original structure and on an original electrochem. signature for stoichiometric Na3V2(PO4)2F3, and we propose that fluctuations in the O/F ratio are at the origin of discrepancies found in the literature.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref28"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref28" class="refNumLink">28</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit28" data-doi="10.1039/C5CP05323D"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bui, K. A polaron-Na vacancy complex model for revealing the diffusion mechanism is proposed for the first time in the field of Na-ion batteries.  The bound polaron is found to favorably form at the first nearest V site to the Na vacancy.  Consequently, the movement of the Na vacancy will be accompanied by the polaron.  Three preferable diffusion pathways are revealed; these are two intra-layer diffusion pathways and one inter-layer pathway.  The activation barriers for the intra-layer and inter-layer pathways are 353 meV and 513 meV, resp.  For further comparison, the generalized gradient approxn. with an on-site Coulomb Hubbard U (GGA + U) is also employed.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref29"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref29" class="refNumLink">29</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit29" data-doi="10.1021/acs.jpcc.8b06120"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, Y.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Concerted Ion-Exchange Mechanism for Sodium Diffusion and Its Promotion in Na3V2(PO4)3 Framework</span>. <i>J. A detailed insight to the transportation behavior of Na+ in the interstitials of NVP lattice should be of great importance for understanding the ionic cond. as well as the electrochem. performances.  Here, we propose 3 different Na diffusion pathways, among which the concerted ion-exchange route is found to be energetically most favorable.  During the migration process, Na ions at both Na(1) and Na(2) sites are engaged in the transportation. It was found that the K-doped NVP exhibits the highest voltage and lowest lattice variation during the charge/discharge process.  The Na diffusion kinetics could be intensively promoted upon K doping.  Our results provide another perspective on the Na migration mechanism in the NVP lattice and suggest that K doping should be a promising soln. to enhance the electrochem. performance of the NVP cathode.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref30"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref30" class="refNumLink">30</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit30" data-doi="10.1039/c4ta00230j"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Song, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ji, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jing, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Banks, C.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The Na superionic conductor (NASICON) Na3V2(PO4)3 is an important pos. electrode material for Na-ion batteries.  Here, we investigate the mechanisms of phase transition in NaxV2(PO4)3 (1 ≤ x ≤ 4) upon nonequil. battery cycling.  Unlike the widely believed two-phase reaction in a Na3V2(PO4)3-Na1V2(PO4)3 system, we det., for the first time, the structure of a recently reported intermediate Na2V2(PO4)3 phase using operando synchrotron X-ray diffraction.  D. functional theory calcns. further support the existence of the Na2V2(PO4)3 phase.  We propose two possible crystal structures of Na2V2(PO4)3 analyzed by Rietveld refinement. The intermediate Na2V2(PO4)3 phase reduces the lattice mismatch between Na3V2(PO4)3 and Na1V2(PO4)3 phases, facilitating a fast phase transition.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Reliable operation of Na-ion batteries requires a mastering of the interfacial processes governing intercalation kinetics.  Ion desolvation, amongst others, is a crucial phenomenon as far as power applications are envisioned.  Studying the intricacies of interfacial processes faces exptl. challenges.  Here the complementary use of electrochem. quartz crystal microbalance (EQCM) and operando IR fiber evanescent wave spectroscopy (IR-FEWS) combined with mol. dynamics (MD) simulations is reported to investigate the electrode/electrolyte interface of polyanionic Na-insertion compds.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Li-ion batteries are the electrochem. energy storage technol. of choice of today's elec. vehicles and grid applications with a growing interest for Na-ion and K-ion systems based on either aq. or non-aq. electrolyte for power, cost, and sustainable reasons.  The rate capability of alkali-metal-ion batteries is influenced by ion transport properties in the bulk of the electrolyte, as well as by diverse effects occurring at the vicinity of the electrode and electrolyte interface.  Therefore, identification of the predominant factor affecting the rate capability of electrodes still remains a challenge and requires suitable exptl. and computational methods.  Herein, we investigate the mechanistic of the K+ insertion process in the Prussian blue phase, in both aq. and non-aq. electrolytes, which reveals drastic differences.  Through combined electrochem. characterizations, electrochem.-quartz-crystal-microbalance and ac-electrogravimetric analyses, we provide evidences that what matters the most for fast ion transport is the positioning of the partially solvated cations adsorbed at the material surface in aq. as opposed to non-aq. electrolytes.  We rationalized such findings by mol. dynamics simulations that establish the K+ repartition profile within the electrochem. double layer.  A similar trend was earlier reported by our group for the aq. vs. non-aq. insertion of Li+ into LiFePO4.  Such a study unveils the crit. but overlooked role of the electrode-electrolyte interface in ruling ion transport and insertion processes.  Tailoring this interface structuring via the proper salt-solvent interaction is the key to enabling the best power performances in alkali-metal-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref38"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref38" class="refNumLink">38</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit38" data-doi="10.1006/jcph.1995.1039"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Plimpton, S.                         cas_cit38R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">38</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Fast parallel algorithms for short-range molecular dynamics</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Plimpton, Steve</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Computational Physics</span>

    </div><div class="casAbstract">Three parallel algorithms for classical mol. dynamics are presented.  The first assigns each processor a fixed subset of atoms; the second assigns each a fixed subset of inter-at. forces to compute; the third assigns each a fixed spatial region.  The algorithms are suitable for mol. dynamics models which can be difficult to parallelize efficiently - those with short-range forces where the neighbors of each atom change rapidly.  They can be implemented on any distributed-memory parallel machine which allows for message-passing of data between independently executing processors.  The algorithms are tested on a std. Lennard-Jones benchmark problem for system sizes ranging from 500 to 100,000,000 atoms on several parallel supercomputers - the nCUBE 2, Intel iPSC/860 and Paragon, and Cray T3D.  Comparing the results to the fastest reported vectorized Cray Y-MP and C90 algorithm shows that the current generation of parallel machines is competitive with conventional vector supercomputers even for small problems.  For large problems, the spatial algorithm achieves parallel efficiencies of 90% and a 1840-node Intel Paragon performs up to 165 faster than a single Cray C90 processor.  Trade-offs between the three algorithms and guidelines for adapting them to more complex mol. dynamics simulations are also discussed.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref39"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref39" class="refNumLink">39</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit39" data-doi="10.1002/jcc.21224"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Martínez, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Andrade, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Birgin, E.                         cas_cit39R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">39</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">PACKMOL: A package for building initial configurations for molecular dynamics simulations</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Martinez, L.; Andrade, R.; Birgin, E. G.; Martinez, J. M.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Computational Chemistry</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2009</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">30</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">13</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2157-2164</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JCCHDD</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0192-8651</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">John Wiley &amp; Sons, Inc.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Adequate initial configurations for mol. dynamics simulations consist of arrangements of mols. distributed in space in such a way to approx. represent the system's overall structure.  In order that the simulations are not disrupted by large van der Waals repulsive interactions, atoms from different mols. must keep safe pairwise distances.  Obtaining such a mol. arrangement can be considered a packing problem: Each type mol. must satisfy spatial constraints related to the geometry of the system, and the distance between atoms of different mols. must be greater than some specified tolerance.  We have developed a code able to pack millions of atoms, grouped in arbitrarily complex mols., inside a variety of three-dimensional regions.  The regions may be intersections of spheres, ellipses, cylinders, planes, or boxes.  The user must provide only the structure of one mol. of each type and the geometrical constraints that each type of mol. must satisfy.  Building complex mixts., interfaces, solvating biomols. in water, other solvents, or mixts. of solvents, is straightforward.  In addn., different atoms belonging to the same mol. may also be restricted to different spatial regions, in such a way that more ordered mol. arrangements can be built, as micelles, lipid double-layers, etc.  The packing time for state-of-the-art mol. dynamics systems varies from a few seconds to a few minutes in a personal computer.  The input files are simple and currently compatible with PDB, Tinker, Molden, or Moldy coordinate files.  The package is distributed as free software and can be downloaded from .  © 2009 Wiley Periodicals, Inc.  J Comput Chem, 2009.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref40"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref40" class="refNumLink">40</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit40" data-doi="10.1107/S0021889808012016"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Momma, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Izumi, F.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">VESTA: A Three-Dimensional Visualization System For Electronic And Structural Analysis</span>. <i>J.                         cas_cit40R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">40</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">VESTA: a three-dimensional visualization system for electronic and structural analysis</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Momma, Koichi; Izumi, Fujio</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Applied Crystallography</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">International Union of Crystallography</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A cross-platform program, VESTA, has been developed to visualize both structural and volumetric data in multiple windows with tabs.  VESTA represents crystal structures by ball-and-stick, space-filling, polyhedral, wire frame, stick, dot-surface and thermal-ellipsoid models.  A variety of crystal-chem. information is extractable from fractional coordinates, occupancies and oxidn. states of sites.  Volumetric data such as electron and nuclear densities, Patterson functions, and wavefunctions are displayed as isosurfaces, bird's-eye views and two-dimensional maps.  Isosurfaces can be colored according to other phys. quantities.  Translucent isosurfaces and/or slices can be overlapped with a structural model.  Collaboration with external programs enables the user to locate bonds and bond angles in the 'graphics area', simulate powder diffraction patterns, and calc. site potentials and Madelung energies.  Electron densities detd. exptl. are convertible into their Laplacians and electronic energy densities.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref41"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref41" class="refNumLink">41</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit41"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Padua, A.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium superionic conductor (NASICON) cathodes are attractive for Na-ion battery applications as they exhibit both high structural stability and high sodium ion mobility.                         cas_cit45R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">45</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Activating a multielectron reaction of NASICON-structured cathodes toward high energy density for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Chen, Mingzhe; Hua, Weibo; Xiao, Jin; Zhang, Jiliang; Lau, Vincent Wing-hei; Park, Mihui; Lee, Gi-Hyeok; Lee, Suwon; Wang, Wanlin; Peng, Jian; Fang, Liang; Zhou, Limin; Chang, Chung-Kai; Yamauchi, Yusuke; Chou, Shulei; Kang, Yong-Mook</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the American Chemical Society</span>
