VMD can simultaneously display any no. of structures using a wide variety of rendering styles and coloring methods.  Mols. are displayed as one or more "representations," in which each representation embodies a particular rendering method and coloring scheme for a selected subset of atoms.  The atoms displayed in each representation are chosen using an extensive atom selection syntax, which includes Boolean operators and regular expressions.  VMD provides a complete graphical user interface for program control, as well as a text interface using the Tcl embeddable parser to allow for complex scripts with variable substitution, control loops, and function calls.  Full session logging is supported, which produces a VMD command script for later playback.  High-resoln. raster images of displayed mols. may be produced by generating input scripts for use by a no. of photorealistic image-rendering applications.  VMD has also been expressly designed with the ability to animate mol. dynamics (MD) simulation trajectories, imported either from files or from a direct connection to a running MD simulation.  VMD is the visualization component of MDScope, a set of tools for interactive problem solving in structural biol., which also includes the parallel MD program NAMD, and the MDCOMM software used to connect the visualization and simulation programs, VMD is written in C++, using an object-oriented design; the program, including source code and extensive documentation, is freely available via anonymous ftp and through the World Wide Web.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref61"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref61" class="rightTabRefNumLink">61</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit61" data-doi="10.1002/jcc.540130812"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kumar, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Rosenberg, J. Method (WHAM), an extension of Ferrenberg and Swendsen's Multiple Histogram Technique, has been applied for the first time on complex biomol. Hamiltonians. This algorithm possesses the following advantages over methods that are currently employed: (1) it provides a built-in est. of sampling errors thereby yielding objective ests. of the optimal location and length of addnl. simulations needed to achieve a desired level of precision; (2) it yields the "best" value of free energies by taking into account all the simulations so as to minimize the statistical errors; (3) in addn. to optimizing the links between simulations, it also allows multiple overlaps of probability distributions for obtaining better ests. of the free-energy differences. Surface-induced structure that extends ∼1 nm from the LiFePO4 surface was obsd. in both electrolytes.  The electrostatic potential at the LiFePO4 surface is neg. relative to the bulk electrolyte reflecting an excess of neg. charge from the electrolyte coordinating surface Li+.  In the ILE system neg. surface charge is partially offset by a high d. of EMIM+ cations coordinating surface oxygen.  The electrostatic potential exhibits a (pos.) max. ∼3 Å from the LiFePO4 surface which, when combined with the reduced ability of the highly structured electrolytes to solvate Li+ cations, results in a free energy barrier of almost 4 kcal mol-1 for penetration of the interfacial electrolyte layer by Li+.  The resistance for bringing Li+ from the bulk electrolyte to the LiFePO4 surface through this interfacial barrier is small for both the OLE and ILE.  However, the ability of EMIM+ cations to donate pos. charge to LiFePO4/electrolyte interface may result in a significant decrease in the concn. of Li+ at the surface and a corresponding increase in impedance to Li+ intercalation into LiFePO4, particularly at lower temps.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref65"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref65" class="rightTabRefNumLink">65</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit65" data-doi="10.1038/s41929-020-0482-5"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dubouis, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Serva, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Berthin, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jeanmairet, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Porcheron, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Salager, E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Salanne, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Grimaud, A.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Tuning Water Reduction through Controlled Nanoconfinement Within an Organic Liquid Matrix</span>. <i>Nat.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The growing hydrogen economy requires accelerating the hydrogen evolution reaction.  The water dissocn. step (Volmer step) has been proposed as a main kinetic limitation, but the mechanisms at play in the electrochem. double-layer are poorly understood.  This is due to the dual role of water: it acts both as a reactant and as a solvent.  Here we propose to confine water inside an org. liq. matrix in order to isolate the sole role of water as a reactant.  We obsd. the formation of aq.-rich nanodomains, whose size can be tuned by changing the supporting electrolyte and found that the reactivity of the system varies significantly with its nanostructure.  Depending on the conditions, the reactivity is dominated by either the strength of short-range cation-water interactions or the formation of long chains of water mols.  Understanding this paves the way towards the development of more efficient and selective electrocatalysts for water, CO2, O2 or N2 redn.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref66"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref66" class="rightTabRefNumLink">66</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit66" data-doi="10.1021/jacs.2c10764"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dorchies, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Serva, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Crevel, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">De Freitas, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kostopoulos, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Robert, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sel, O.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Salanne, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Grimaud, A.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Controlling the Hydrophilicity of the Electrochemical Interface to Modulate the Oxygen-Atom Transfer in Electrocatalytic Epoxidation Reactions</span>. <i>J. Am. Chem. Soc.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2022</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>144</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">22734</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">22746</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1021/jacs.2c10764</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit66R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">66</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Controlling the Hydrophilicity of the Electrochemical Interface to Modulate the Oxygen-Atom Transfer in Electrocatalytic Epoxidation Reactions</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Dorchies, Florian; Serva, Alessandra; Crevel, Dorian; De Freitas, Jeremy; Kostopoulos, Nikolaos; Robert, Marc; Sel, Ozlem; Salanne, Mathieu; Grimaud, Alexis</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the American Chemical Society</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2022</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">144</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">49</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">22734-22746</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JACSAT</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0002-7863</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The electro catalytic epoxidn. of alkenes at heterogeneous catalysts using water as the sole oxygen source is a promising safe route toward the sustainable synthesis of epoxides, which are essential building blocks in org. chem.  However, the physicochem. parameters governing the oxygen-atom transfer to the alkene and the impact of the electrolyte structure on the epoxidn. reaction are yet to be understood.  Here, we study the electro catalytic epoxidn. of cyclooctene at the surface of gold in hybrid org./aq. mixts. using acetonitrile (ACN) solvent.  Gold was selected, as in ACN/water electrolytes gold oxide is formed by reactivity with water at potentials less anodic than the oxygen evolution reaction (OER).  This unique property allows us to demonstrate that a sacrificial mechanism is responsible for cyclooctene epoxidn. at metallic gold surfaces, proceeding through cyclooctene activation, while epoxidn. at gold oxide shares similar reaction intermediates with the OER and proceeds via the activation of water.  More importantly, we show that the hydrophilicity of the electrode/electrolyte interface can be tuned by changing the nature of the supporting salt cation, hence affecting the reaction selectivity.  At low over potential, hydrophilic interfaces formed using strong Lewis acid cations are found to favor gold passivation.  Instead, hydrophobic interfaces created by the use of large org. cations favor the oxidn. of cyclooctene and the formation of epoxide.  Our study directly demonstrates how tuning the hydrophilicity of electrochem. interfaces can improve both the yield and selectivity of anodic reactions at the surface of heterogeneous catalysts.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref67"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref67" class="rightTabRefNumLink">67</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit67" data-doi="10.1039/C6CP05140E"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Boyer, M. J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Vilčiauskas, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hwang, G. S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Structure and Li+ Ion Transport in A Mixed Carbonate/Lipf6 Electrolyte Near Graphite Electrode Surfaces: A Molecular Dynamics Study</span>. <i>Phys. Chem. Chem. Phys.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2016</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>18</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">27868</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">27876</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1039/C6CP05140E</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit67R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">67</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Structure and Li+ ion transport in a mixed carbonate/LiPF6 electrolyte near graphite electrode surfaces: a molecular dynamics study</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Boyer, Mathew J.; Vilciauskas, Linas; Hwang, Gyeong S.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Physical Chemistry Chemical Physics</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">18</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">40</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">27868-27876</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">PPCPFQ</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1463-9076</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Electrolyte and electrode materials used in lithium-ion batteries have been studied sep. to a great extent, however the structural and dynamical properties of the electrolyte-electrode interface still remain largely unexplored despite its crit. role in governing battery performance.  Using mol. dynamics simulations, we examine the structural reorganization of solvent mols. (cyclic ethylene carbonate : linear di-Me carbonate 1 : 1 molar ratio doped with 1 M LiPF6) in the vicinity of graphite electrodes with varying surface charge densities (σ).  The interfacial structure is found to be sensitive to the mol. geometry and polarity of each solvent mol. as well as the surface structure and charge distribution of the neg. electrode.  We also evaluated the p.d. across the electrolyte-electrode interface, which exhibits a nearly linear variation with respect to σ up until the onset of Li+ ion accumulation onto the graphite edges from the electrolyte.  In addn., well-tempered metadynamics simulations are employed to predict the free-energy barriers to Li+ ion transport through the relatively dense interfacial layer, along with anal. of the Li+ solvation sheath structure.  Quant. anal. of the mol. arrangements at the electrolyte-electrode interface will help better understand and describe electrolyte decompn., esp. in the early stages of solid-electrolyte-interphase (SEI) formation.  Moreover, the computational framework presented in this work offers a means to explore the effects of solvent compn., electrode surface modification, and operating temp. on the interfacial structure and properties, which may further assist in efforts to engineer the electrolyte-electrode interface leading to a SEI layer that optimizes battery performance.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref68"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref68" class="rightTabRefNumLink">68</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit68" data-doi="10.1021/jp2101539"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Vatamanu, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Borodin, O.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Smith, G. D.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Molecular Dynamics Simulation Studies of the Structure of a Mixed Carbonate/LiPF6 Electrolyte near Graphite Surface as a Function of Electrode Potential</span>. <i>J. Phys. Chem. C</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2012</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>116</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">1114</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">1121</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1021/jp2101539</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit68R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">68</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Molecular dynamics simulation studies of the structure of a mixed carbonate/LiPF6 electrolyte near graphite surface as a function of electrode potential</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Vatamanu, Jenel; Borodin, Oleg; Smith, Grant D.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Physical Chemistry C</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2012</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">116</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1114-1121</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JPCCCK</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1932-7447</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Mol. dynamics (MD) simulations of an electrolyte comprised of ethylene carbonate (EC), di-Me carbonate (DMC), and LiPF6 salt near the basal face of graphite electrodes have been performed as a function of electrode potential.  Upon charging of the electrodes, the less polar DMC mol. is partially replaced in the interfacial electrolyte layer by the more polar EC.  At neg. potentials, the carbonyl groups from the carbonate mols. are repelled from the surface, while at pos. potentials, we find a substantial enrichment of the surface with carbonyl groups.  PF6- rapidly accumulates at the pos. electrode with increasing potential and vacates the neg. electrode with increasing neg. potential.  In contrast, Li+ concn. in the interfacial layer is found to be only weakly dependent on potential except at very large neg. potentials.  Hence, both compn. of the electrolyte at the electrode surface and solvent environment around Li+ are obsd. to vary dramatically with the applied potential with important implications for oxidn./redn. of the electrolyte and the process of Li+ intercalation/deintercation.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref69"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref69" class="rightTabRefNumLink">69</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit69" data-doi="10.1021/la901829v"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yamada, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Iriyama, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Abe, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ogumi, Z.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Kinetics of Lithium Ion Transfer at the Interface between Graphite and Liquid Electrolytes: Effects of Solvent and Surface Film</span>. <i>Langmuir</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2009</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>25</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">12766</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">12770</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1021/la901829v</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit69R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">69</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Kinetics of Lithium Ion Transfer at the Interface between Graphite and Liquid Electrolytes: Effects of Solvent and Surface Film</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yamada, Yuki; Iriyama, Yasutoshi; Abe, Takeshi; Ogumi, Zempachi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Langmuir</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2009</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">25</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">21</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">12766-12770</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">LANGD5</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0743-7463</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The kinetics of Li ion transfer at an interface between graphite and liq. electrolyte was studied by a.c. impedance spectroscopy.  Using highly oriented pyrolytic graphite (HOPG) as a model electrode, the authors evaluated the activation energies of the interfacial Li ion transfer from the temp. dependences of the interfacial conductivities.  When a binary electrolyte consisting of LiClO4 dissolved in a mixt. of ethylene carbonate (EC) and di-Me carbonate (DMC) (1:1 by vol.) was used, the activation energy of the interfacial Li ion transfer was 58 kJ mol-1, while an electrolyte consisting of LiClO4 dissolved in DMC gave an activation energy of 40 kJ mol-1.  A calcn. with the d. functional theory clarified that the solvation ability of EC is higher than that of DMC.  Therefore, the activation energies of the interfacial Li ion transfer at graphite reflected the energies for the desolvation of Li ion from the solvent mol.  Also, the activation energies of the interfacial Li ion transfer varied in the presence of different surface films (solid electrolyte interphase, SEI).  Probably the kinetics of the interfacial Li ion transfer at graphite is influenced by the compns. of SEI films as well as the desolvation of Li ion from solvent mols.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref70"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref70" class="rightTabRefNumLink">70</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit70" data-doi="10.1016/j.electacta.2021.137843"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chekushkin, P. M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Merenkov, I. S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Smirnov, V. S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kislenko, S. A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nikitina, V. A.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">The Physical Origin of the Activation Barrier in Li-Ion Intercalation Processes: The Overestimated Role of Desolvation</span>. <i>Electrochim. Acta</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2021</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>372</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">137843</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/j.electacta.2021.137843</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit70R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">70</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">The physical origin of the activation barrier in Li-ion intercalation processes: the overestimated role of desolvation</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Chekushkin, Petr M.; Merenkov, Ivan S.; Smirnov, Vladimir S.; Kislenko, Sergey A.; Nikitina, Victoria A.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Electrochimica Acta</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2021</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">372</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">137843</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ELCAAV</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0013-4686</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The energetically hindered step of Li-ion desolvation in ion intercalation into cathode or anode materials for Li-ion batteries is frequently considered to be responsible for the pronounced rate-limitations in the low-temp. and high-power limits of battery operation.  The authors provide strong evidence for an alternative structure of the intercalation activation barrier, with the rate-limiting step being dependent on both the solvent and the electrode material nature.  The authors combine the exptl. measurements of the activation energies for LiCoO2 and LiMn2O4 electrodes in aq., carbonate-, MeCN-, and diglyme-based electrolytes with classical mol. dynamics simulations and conclude that ionic desolvation can become a rate-limiting step only in cases when resistive layers (commonly referred to as a Cathode/Electrolyte Interface) do not form at the surface of the electrode materials.  In conventional carbonate-based electrolytes, the rate-limiting step is assocd. with the electrochem. step of ion transfer across the electrode/electrolyte interface.  The results of this study reveal the overestimated role of desolvation in ion intercalation processes.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li></ol></li><li id="pane-m3" aria-labelledby="pane-m3" class="tab__pane tab__pane-suppInfo"><h2 class="supp-info-label">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article_supporting-info" id="rightTab-notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="rightTab-_i7">Supporting Information</h2></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsami.3c12815?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsami.3c12815</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Density profiles, probability contours, simulation snapshots, and coordination number plots (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c12815/suppl_file/am3c12815_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c12815/suppl_file/am3c12815_si_001.pdf">am3c12815_si_001.pdf (2.31 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div><div id="rightTab-figshare-widget"></div><br><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzoneRight" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzoneRight"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="b84e6b6c-b4d0-4427-b286-3cf14d52d013" class="mb-4">

                <a title="1.  Introduction" class="article__jump__link internalNav" href="#_i2" data-id="article_stickyheader_JumpTo_1.%20%20Introduction">

                <a title="2.  Methods" class="article__jump__link internalNav" href="#_i3" data-id="article_stickyheader_JumpTo_2.%20%20Methods">
                   <span>2.  Methods</span>
                </a>
            </li>
            <li>
                <a title="3.  Results and Discussion" class="article__jump__link internalNav" href="#_i5" data-id="article_stickyheader_JumpTo_3.%20%20Results%20and%20Discussion">
                   <span>3.  Results and Discussion</span>
                </a>
            </li>
            <li>
                <a title="4.  Conclusions" class="article__jump__link internalNav" href="#_i6" data-id="article_stickyheader_JumpTo_4.%20%20Conclusions">
                   <span>4.  Conclusions</span>
                </a>

        <script nonce="931883a74ad354b8-SJC">
(function(){
	
	/*Event Tracking for RIS menu dropdown - Article pages*/

	$('.cit-download-dropdown_button').on('click',function(e)
	{	
		e.preventDefault();
	    gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':'Main'});

	});


	$('.cit-download-dropdown_content ul li').on('click',function(e)
	{	
	//	e.preventDefault();
	    var idx = $(this).index();
	    
	    var label = '';
	    if (idx === 0 ) {
	    	label = 'Citation';
	    } else if (idx === 1 ) {
	    	label = 'Abstract';
	    } else if (idx === 2 ) {
	    	label = 'Reference';
	    } else if (idx === 3 ) {
	    	label = 'More Options';
	    }
	   
	   gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':label});
	});

	$('.header_contnav-next a').on('click', function(e){
        e.preventDefault();
        gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Right Arrow'});
	}); 

 	$('.header_contnav-pre a').on('click', function(e){
	    e.preventDefault();
	     gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Left Arrow'});
	}); 

	$('.share_button').on('click', function(e){
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Share'});
	});

	$("#pane-m1con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Figures'});
	});

	$("#pane-m2con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
	    gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'References'});
	});

	$("#pane-m3con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Supporting Info'});
	});

	$('.tab__close').on('click', function(){
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Close'});
	}); 



})();

</script>

<script nonce="931883a74ad354b8-SJC">
document.addEventListener('DOMContentLoaded', function () {
    const modalContainer = document.querySelector('.my-main-container');

    // Function to show the modal
    function showModal() {
        modalContainer.style.display = 'flex';
        document.querySelector('.overlay').style.display = 'block';
    }

    // Function to hide the modal when the close button is clicked
    function hideModal() {
        modalContainer.style.display = 'none';
        document.querySelector('.overlay').style.display = 'none';
    }

    // Hide the modal initially
    hideModal();

    // Function to handle clicks outside the modal
    function handleClickOutside(event) {
        // const modalContainer = document.querySelector(".my-main-container");
        // Check if the click target is not a child of the modal container
        if (!modalContainer.contains(event.target)) {
            hideModal();
        }
    }

    // Add a click event listener to the close button
    document
        .querySelector('.close-button')
        .addEventListener('click', hideModal);

    // Add a click event listener to the document to handle clicks outside the modal
    document.addEventListener('click', handleClickOutside);

    const screenHeight = window.innerHeight;
    function updateModalPosition() {
        const scrollY = window.scrollY;
        // console.log("scrollY= " + scrollY);
        // modalContainer.style.top = `${50 + scrollY}px`;
        modalContainer.style.top = `${scrollY + screenHeight / 2}px`;
        // console.log("new top= " + modalContainer.style.top);
    }

    // Update the modal position when the user scrolls
    window.addEventListener('scroll', updateModalPosition);

    // Initialize the modal position
    updateModalPosition();

    //---Cookie---
    // Function to set the cookie with a given name, value, and expiration time in days
    function setCookie(name, value, days) {
        var expires = '';
        if (days) {
            var date = new Date();
            date.setTime(date.getTime() + days * 24 * 60 * 60 * 1000);
            expires = '; expires=' + date.toUTCString();
            console.log(expires + 'this is expires dateUTC');
        }
        document.cookie = name + '=' + (value || '') + expires + '; path=/';
        // console.log("document.cookie is: " + document.cookie);
    }

    if (!document.cookie.includes('modalAd')) {
        // Show Modal
        showModal();

        // Set the cookie with the name "modalAd", value "true", and expiration of 3 day
        setCookie('modalAd', 'true', 3);
    } else {
        hideModal();
    }
});

</script>
<link rel="stylesheet" type="text/css" href="/pb-assets/smartgroup_ads/Modal-LeadGen-1691422642793.css">
            
        </div>

<script nonce="931883a74ad354b8-SJC">
    document.addEventListener("DOMContentLoaded", function(){
    // Function to get a Human readable formatted date
    function getFormattedDateForMaintenance() {
        let today = new Date();
        let dd = String(today.getDate()).padStart(2, '0');
        //January is 0
        let mm = String(today.getMonth() + 1).padStart(2, '0'); 
        let yyyy = today.getFullYear();
        
        let daysOfWeek = ['Sunday', 'Monday', 'Tuesday', 'Wednesday', 'Thursday', 'Friday', 'Saturday'];
        let monthsOfYear = ['January', 'February', 'March', 'April', 'May', 'June', 'July', 'August', 'September', 'October', 'November', 'December'];

        let dayOfWeek = daysOfWeek[today.getDay()];
        let monthOfYear = monthsOfYear[today.getMonth()];

        return dayOfWeek + ', ' + monthOfYear + ' ' + dd + ', ' + yyyy;
    }
    
    // Insert the date into the .system-message-date element
    document.querySelector('.system-message-date').innerText = getFormattedDateForMaintenance();
});
</script>

      function init() {
        setTimeout(function () {
          var cookieExist = getCookie("gdpr");
          if (cookieExist) {
            //console.log("gdpr cookie exist");
            return false;
          } else {
            //console.log("gdpr cookie does not exist")
            gdprMessage();

        $("#gdpr-message-modal").show();

      function handleClick() {
        //console.log($("#gdpr-input").val());
        emailValid = validateEmail($("#gdpr-input").val());
        if (emailValid) {
          //Submit the form
          //Hide form
          if ($(".gdpr_err")) {
            $(".gdpr_err").hide();
          }

          $("#gdpr-message-content");
          //If successful then...
          setCookie("gdpr", "true");

          //console.log("emailValid " + emailValid);
        }

      function gdprErrorMessage() {
        if (errorStatus) {
          return false;

          errorStatus = true;

      function validateEmail(email) {
        var re =
          /^(([^<>()\[\]\\.,;:\s@"]+(\.[^<>()\[\]\\.,;:\s@"]+)*)|(".+"))@((\[[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\])|(([a-zA-Z\-0-9]+\.)+[a-zA-Z]{2,}))$/;
        return re.test(String(email).toLowerCase());
      }

      function setCookie(name, value) {
        var expires = "";
        expires = "; expires=Fri, 31 Dec 9999 23:59:59 GMT";
        document.cookie = name + "=" + (value || "") + expires + "; path=/";
      }

      function getCookie(name) {
        var match = document.cookie.match(new RegExp(name + "=([^;]+)"));
        if (match) return match[1];
      }

      function hideForm() {
        //And set cookie
        $("#gdpr-message-modal").hide();
        setCookie("gdpr", "false");
        gtag("event", "GDPR Modal", {
          event_category: "Pubs GDPR",
          event_label: "GDPR Dismissal",
        });
      }

      return {
        init: init,
      };
    })();

  </script>

        <script nonce="931883a74ad354b8-SJC">
var currentJournal = "ACS Applied Materials &amp;amp; Interfaces";
$(".citationLinks > a").click(function(e){
    if($(this).text() === "ACS Full Text ") {
        gtag('event', 'ACS Full Text', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "Crossref") {
        gtag('event', 'Crossref', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "CAS") {
        gtag('event', 'CAS', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "PubMed") {
        gtag('event', 'PubMed', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "Google Scholar") {
        gtag('event', 'Google Scholar', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).hasClass("issue-item_open-url") === true) {
        gtag('event', 'OpenURL', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
});
</script>

    console.log("ACSPubs2 start");
    $.getJSON('https://extreme-ip-lookup.com/json/', function(data, status) {
            if ( status == 'success' ) {
                $.each(data, function(i, field){
                    cookie=cookie.concat(i+' = '+field+" - ");

                    cookie=status;

            cookie1 = cookie;

            $.cookie("ACSPubs2", btoa(cookie), {path: '/'}); // Session cookie valid across the entire site.
            //TODO send cookie val to the controller for logging
            console.log("ACSPubs2 success");
    })
    .fail(function(xx, status, err){
